```{=html}
<title>
```
ABSTRACT
```{=html}
</title>
```
`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0001">`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Aim
```{=html}
</title>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
The heterogeneous metrics and criteria used to assess the effectiveness
of substance use disorders treatment hinders cross‐study comparisons.
This review aims to parse such heterogeneity by analysing the
operational definitions of variables used to derive metrics and outcome
criteria, contributing to the standardisation process.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0002">`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Methods
```{=html}
</title>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
We conducted a systematic review in PubMed and PsycINFO between January
2000 and October 2023. We included published studies on substance use
disorders that featured at least one of seven 'a priori' defined
variables commonly used to obtain metrics and criteria for treatment
effectiveness. The review process and reporting followed Preferred
Reporting Items for Systematic Reviews and Meta‐Analysis (PRISMA)
guidelines.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0003">`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Results
```{=html}
</title>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
Were identified three areas that can be used to define metrics and
criteria associated with treatment outcome: as 'substance use'
(abstinence and relapses), 'treatment process' (readmission, dropout,
retention, and adherence) and 'general wellbeing' (quality of life).
Operational definitions and metrics and criteria used were overall
inconsistent.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0004">`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Conclusions
```{=html}
</title>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
The establishment of guidelines for evaluation of treatment outcomes is
imperative, as heterogeneity is still present in the literature. We
recommend that future trials provide outcomes metrics relevant to the
identified categories, and that standardisation efforts continue toward
harmonised criteria to report and interpret those metrics.
```{=html}
</p>
```

```{=html}
<body id="mpr70027-body-0001">
```
`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0010">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}1`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Introduction
```{=html}
</title>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
Substance use disorders (SUDs) represent a pervasive and critical global
health challenge, consistently ranking as one of the leading causes of
morbidity and mortality (Degenhardt
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0037" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2017`</xref>`{=html}).
According to the United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime World Drug
Report (United Nations Office on Drugs and
Crime `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0166" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2024`</xref>`{=html}),
in 2022, approximately 64 million people worldwide suffered from SUD,
equivalent to 1 in 81 people. This figure represents a 3% increase
compared with that in 2018. Therefore, the need for evidence‐based
treatment is noteworthy (Pickering
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0136" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2017`</xref>`{=html}).
Evidence on treatment efficacy is mixed, while some studies have shown
that more than half of patients seem to be in recovery (Jones
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0076" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2020`</xref>`{=html}),
other studies have reported recovery rates lower than 10% (Kelly
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0081" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2017`</xref>`{=html}).
These inconsistent findings could be due to what it means to be 'in
recovery' or 'treatment effectiveness' (W. L.
White `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0180" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2007`</xref>`{=html}).
```{=html}
</p>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
As in other mental health interventions, outcome measurement,
encompassing metrics and criteria, is crucial for determining the
effectiveness of interventions. Since the 1990s, numerous studies on the
effectiveness and efficacy of treatments have been conducted using a
wide range of outcome variables (Alves
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0005" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2017`</xref>`{=html}).
Historically, SUD research has prioritised substance use terms---such as
abstinence‐ for evaluating treatment. However, in alignment with
harm‐reduction models ‐ an approach aimed at minimising the adverse
consequences of drug use, accepting that some users will not cease
consumption
(Single `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0149" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}1995`</xref>`{=html})‐
some authors point out that a more comprehensive view extends beyond the
frequency and quantity of use (Tiffany
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0161" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2012`</xref>`{=html}).
This paradigm has been recognised by regulatory agencies such as, the
Food and Drug Administration (FDA), which has shown a keen interest in
developing no‐abstinence‐based endpoints (Kiluk
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0083" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2019`</xref>`{=html})
or assessing the broader negative consequences of drug use. In addition,
different studies have also highlighted the need to analyze variables
related to the therapeutic process (Penzenstadler
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0132" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2017`</xref>`{=html}).
Those, such as retention or adherence to treatment, are associated with
long‐term outcomes such as, reduced drug use, therapeutic success,
improved quality of life (QoL), or a reduced likelihood of future drug
use problems (Gaulen
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0061" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html}).
Consequently, researchers and clinicians are embracing this novel
paradigm, incorporating variables such as QoL and other treatment
process indicators.
```{=html}
</p>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
Based on the aforementioned arguments, the utilisation of substance use,
QoL, and treatment process variables to inform the development of
outcome metrics and criteria to define effectiveness is a practice that
has gained considerable traction within the scientific literature,
particularly in the context of evaluating treatment outcomes
(Dacosta‐Sánchez
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0033" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html};
Tiffany
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0161" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2012`</xref>`{=html}).
However, 2 decades prior, W. White and
Godley (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0179" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2005`</xref>`{=html})
highlighted the lack of consensus in the standardisation of outcome
measures (i.e., metrics and criteria) used to determine treatment
outcomes. These problems make cross‐study comparisons difficult and can
limit the use of meta‐analytical techniques (Donovan
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0041" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2012`</xref>`{=html}).
Consequently, the lack of certainty could limit the ability of
healthcare professionals to make scientific‐based informed decisions
(Karnik
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0079" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html}).
```{=html}
</p>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
Therefore, given the substantial heterogeneity in the current
literature, there is a need to harmonise the metrics and criteria used
to SUD treatment outcomes to enable comparability across studies. To
progress toward this goal, we conducted a systematic review covering
seven variables commonly used in the specialised literature to define
metrics and criteria for outcome evaluation, namely abstinence,
retention (Wiessing
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0181" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2018`</xref>`{=html}),
adherence/engagement (S. Reif
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0140" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2021`</xref>`{=html}),
dropout (H. H. Brorson
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0024" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2013`</xref>`{=html}),
quality of life (Bratu
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0022" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2023`</xref>`{=html}),
readmission (Böckmann
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0019" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2019`</xref>`{=html};
Donisi
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0040" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2016`</xref>`{=html}),
and relapse (Moe
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0116" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html}).
Our review focused on addressing the following questions: What metrics
are used to operationalise these variables, and what criteria are
applied to evaluate treatment effectiveness based on those metrics?
Accordingly, our main objectives were to examine the literature: (i) to
analyze the operational definitions of the seven 'a priori' defined
variables and (ii) to examine the metrics and criteria used to evaluate
and determine intervention outcomes.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0020">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}2`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Methods
```{=html}
</title>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
A systematic literature review was conducted using the Preferred
Reporting Items for Systematic Reviews and Meta‐Analysis (PRISMA)
guidelines (Page
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0129" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2021`</xref>`{=html}).
The systematic review protocol was pre‐registered in the International
Prospective Register of Systematic Reviews (PROSPERO) under the
registration code CRD42024500356.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0030">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}2.1`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Search Strategy
```{=html}
</title>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
Searches were conducted on 18 October 2023 in two widely used databases
for health and behavioral sciences (Falagas
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0049" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2008`</xref>`{=html}):
PubMed and PsycINFO. The search was limited to articles published
between January 1, 2000 and search date, to capture contemporary
research. Additionally, it was restricted to studies involving the adult
population, ensuring sample homogeneity. The search strategy was limited
to the title field and included (Addiction OR Substance use disorder OR
Substance abuse disorder) AND (Abstinence OR Adherence OR Dropout OR
Engagement OR Quality of Life OR Relapse OR Retention OR Readmission).
These terms were selected for their common use in the literature for
evaluating treatment effectiveness in patients with SUD and have been
frequently analysed in prior studies (Böckmann
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0019" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2019`</xref>`{=html};
Bratu
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0022" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2023`</xref>`{=html};
H. H. Brorson
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0024" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2013`</xref>`{=html};
Donisi
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0040" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2016`</xref>`{=html};
Moe
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0116" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html};
S. Reif
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0140" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2021`</xref>`{=html};
Wiessing
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0181" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2018`</xref>`{=html}).
The concept of engagement was included because, in the literature,
researchers use both 'engagement to treatment' and 'treatment adherence'
synonymously (Morandi
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0119" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2017`</xref>`{=html}).
```{=html}
</p>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
All initial results were exported from their databases to the systematic
review software site Rayyan (Ouzzani
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0127" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2016`</xref>`{=html}),
yielding a total of 1068 publications. A first screening based on title
and abstract was conducted, applying the following exclusion
criteria:`<list list-type="order">`{=html}`<list-item>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
Gray literature (to ensure the inclusion of peer‐reviewed empirical
studies).
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</list-item>`{=html}`<list-item>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
Non‐English full text available.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</list-item>`{=html}`<list-item>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
Studies include participants under 18 years old.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</list-item>`{=html}`<list-item>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
Studies focusing on tobacco or behavioral addictions. Research related
to tobacco use disorder was excluded to maintain methodological
consistency. This is because such studies frequently utilise disparate
outcome measures (e.g., abstinence as the sole metric) and are often
conducted in distinct treatment settings, such as primary care, which
differ from those employed for other SUD.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</list-item>`{=html}`<list-item>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
Case reports.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</list-item>`{=html}`<list-item>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
Systematic reviews.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</list-item>`{=html}`</list>`{=html}
```{=html}
</p>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
After this initial screening, 407 papers underwent full‐text screening
by one of the authors to determine which ones met selection criteria.
Two other authors contributed to resolve uncertain cases and agreed on
the final selection of articles. The inclusion criteria were defined as
follows:`<list list-type="order">`{=html}`<list-item>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
The sample consisted of individuals diagnosed with SUD who received
treatment in any modality (outpatient or inpatient).
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</list-item>`{=html}`<list-item>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
The study examined at least one of the following variables: adherence,
engagement, relapse, readmission, retention, QoL, dropout, or
abstinence. Studies investigating risk factors for one of the previous
variables using at least two time‐point measures (pre‐post) of the
outcome.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</list-item>`{=html}`</list>`{=html}
```{=html}
</p>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
Finally, 110 studies were included in the review.
Figure `<xref rid="mpr70027-fig-0001" ref-type="fig">`{=html}1`</xref>`{=html}
describes the process of selection checking according to PRISMA.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`<fig position="float" fig-type="FIGURE" id="mpr70027-fig-0001" orientation="portrait">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}FIGURE
1`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<caption>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
PRISMA flow diagram.
```{=html}
</p>
```
```{=html}
</caption>
```
`<graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" id="jats-graphic-1" orientation="portrait" xlink:href="MPR-34-e70027-g001.jpg"/>`{=html}`</fig>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
The extracted data are summarised in three categories. The substance
use‐related variables included 'abstinence' and 'relapse.' The treatment
process‐related variables were 'dropout,' 'retention,' 'readmission,'
and 'engagement'. Lastly, the third category included 'quality of life'.
To facilitate identification of the studies included in the systematic
review, those that met the inclusion criteria have been marked with an
asterisk (\*) in the reference section.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</sec>`{=html}`</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0040">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}3`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Results
```{=html}
</title>
```
`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0050">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}3.1`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Variables Related to Substance Use
```{=html}
</title>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
A total of 44 studies included variables related to substance use, with
20 using 'abstinence' and 22 'relapse'
(Tables `<xref rid="mpr70027-tbl-0001" ref-type="table">`{=html}1`</xref>`{=html}
and
`<xref rid="mpr70027-tbl-0002" ref-type="table">`{=html}2`</xref>`{=html}).
```{=html}
</p>
```

Variables related to substance use‐relapse.

`</table-wrap>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
Regarding the operational definitions of these terms, most of the
studies (`<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}n`</italic>`{=html} = 16) defined
abstinence based on reported use of alcohol or illicit drugs, applying
the criteria of no reported used. In line with this, for relapse, nine
of the 22 studies considered zero reported consumption as the criterion
for 'no relapse'. However, 13 studies differ from that. For example,
Palma‐Álvarez
et al. (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0130" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2021`</xref>`{=html})
defined the criteria for relapse as 'three consecutive positive
urinalyses of the substance that the patient used in the first instance
or the more problematic one'. Additionally, two of the studies
considered being readmitted to treatment as relapse
(Nordfjærn `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0122" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2011`</xref>`{=html};
Waite
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0173" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2023`</xref>`{=html}).
```{=html}
</p>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
The literature also reflects the distinction between 'slip' and
'relapse', with five studies addressing this. For instance, Krupitsky
et al. (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0088" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2006`</xref>`{=html})
defined relapse using three different criteria: reported daily heroin
use, three consecutive positive tests or the presence of withdrawal
symptoms, and a slip as: reported occasional heroin use, less than three
consecutive opioid‐positive urine tests and no withdrawal symptoms.
```{=html}
</p>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
For both variables, the main type of data collection was self‐reports.
Among the studies including abstinence, a wide variety of instruments
have been used, such as the `<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}Addiction
Severity Index`</italic>`{=html} (ASI) (McLellan
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0114" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}1992`</xref>`{=html}),
its European version (EuropASI) (Blacken
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0018" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}1994`</xref>`{=html}),
the `<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}Timeline Follow‐back`</italic>`{=html}
(TLFB) (Sobell and
Sobell `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0153" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}1992`</xref>`{=html}),
the `<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}Health and Daily Living
Form`</italic>`{=html} (Moos
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0118" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}1990`</xref>`{=html}),
`<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}Treatment Outcomes Prospective Studies
inventories`</italic>`{=html} (Hubbard
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0071" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}1989`</xref>`{=html}),
and ad‐hoc interviews. For relapse, only the TLFB (Sobell and
Sobell `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0153" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}1992`</xref>`{=html})
and ad‐hoc interviews were used. The TLFB was the most used measure for
both variables
(Figure `<xref rid="mpr70027-fig-0002" ref-type="fig">`{=html}2`</xref>`{=html}).
Regarding biological measures, nine of the studies on abstinence and 11
of the relapse papers included urine, exhaled air, or blood samples.
Fourteen studies combined both measures (i.e., biological and
self‐reported). Two studies (Mattson
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0110" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2010`</xref>`{=html};
Norman
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0123" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2007`</xref>`{=html})
used a collateral informant measure (e.g., family or friends).
```{=html}
</p>
```
`<fig position="float" fig-type="FIGURE" id="mpr70027-fig-0002" orientation="portrait">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}FIGURE
2`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<caption>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
Assessment period for substance use measures.
```{=html}
</p>
```
```{=html}
</caption>
```
`<graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" id="jats-graphic-3" orientation="portrait" xlink:href="MPR-34-e70027-g003.jpg"/>`{=html}`</fig>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
The observed period varied widely, ranging from daily measures to 1 year
after treatment onset. Most commonly, the last 30 days
(`<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}n`</italic>`{=html} = 11) or the last
3 months (`<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}n`</italic>`{=html} = 11) were
used to assess substance use
(Figure `<xref rid="mpr70027-fig-0003" ref-type="fig">`{=html}3`</xref>`{=html}).
```{=html}
</p>
```
`<fig position="float" fig-type="FIGURE" id="mpr70027-fig-0003" orientation="portrait">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}FIGURE
3`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<caption>
```
```{=html}

```
Scales used for substance use measures.
```{=html}
</p>
```

```{=html}
</caption>
```

`<graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" id="jats-graphic-5" orientation="portrait" xlink:href="MPR-34-e70027-g004.jpg"/>`{=html}`</fig>`{=html}`</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0060">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}3.2`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Variables Related to the Treatment Process
```{=html}
</title>
```
`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0070">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}3.2.1`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Readmission
```{=html}
</title>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
Eight papers
(Table `<xref rid="mpr70027-tbl-0003" ref-type="table">`{=html}3`</xref>`{=html})
examined readmission rates, all conducted in hospital settings.
Regarding the operational definition, five studies considered
readmission as a new substance‐related or psychiatric admission
following initial discharge. In contrast, three of them reflected any
new hospitalisation as the criteria to consider the patient as
readmitted (e.g., emergency admissions). Only Gryczynski
et al. (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0066" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2021`</xref>`{=html})
and Maturana
et al. (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0111" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2023`</xref>`{=html})
analysed data from a large setting of treatments.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`<table-wrap position="float" id="mpr70027-tbl-0003" content-type="TABLE" orientation="portrait">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}TABLE
3`</label>`{=html}

```{=html}
</table>
```
`<table-wrap-foot id="mpr70027-ntgp-0001">`{=html}`<fn id="mpr70027-note-0002">`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
Abbreviation: SUD: Substance Use Disorder.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</fn>`{=html}`</table-wrap-foot>`{=html}`</table-wrap>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
In terms of the follow‐up period, there was a great variety, ranging
from 30 days after discharge to 9 years after discharge. 1‐month or
6‐month periods were the most common.
```{=html}
</p>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
Concerning the metric of the variable, five studies dichotomised it
(readmission vs. no readmission) based on previously established
criteria for analysing readmission rates. Three studies examined the
time until new readmission, and only one analysed the number of days
spent in the hospital after discharge.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0080">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}3.2.2`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Dropout
```{=html}
</title>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
Dropout was reported in 24 studies
(Table `<xref rid="mpr70027-tbl-0004" ref-type="table">`{=html}4`</xref>`{=html}).
Dropout was usually operationalised as 'stopping treatment before
clinical recommendation'. However, specific criteria varied, from
'failure to return for the next session' (Jarnecke
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0074" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2019`</xref>`{=html})
to 'being in treatment for less than 6 months' (Padyab
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0128" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2015`</xref>`{=html}).
Two of these studies (Tull and
Gratz `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0164" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2012`</xref>`{=html};
Tull
et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0165" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2013`</xref>`{=html})
considered discharge due to disciplinary infractions as dropping out of
treatment.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`<table-wrap position="float" id="mpr70027-tbl-0004" content-type="TABLE" orientation="portrait">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}TABLE
4`</label>`{=html}


```{=html}

```
```{=html}
</tbody>
```
```{=html}
</table>
```
`</table-wrap>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
Regarding measurement concerns, none of the studies reported a specific
metric; instead, they dichotomised the variable, to enable calculation
of dropout rates. The dropout rates ranged from 12% to 73%. Follow‐up
periods ranged from 30 days to 5 years, with 3 months being the most
common.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0090">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}3.2.3`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Retention
```{=html}
</title>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
Of the 23 papers reviewed that included retention
(Table `<xref rid="mpr70027-tbl-0005" ref-type="table">`{=html}5`</xref>`{=html}),
11 focused on residential treatment settings.

`<table-wrap-foot id="mpr70027-ntgp-0002">`{=html}`<fn id="mpr70027-note-0003">`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
Abbreviation: SUD: substance use disorder.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</fn>`{=html}`</table-wrap-foot>`{=html}`</table-wrap>`{=html}
```{=html}
<p>
```
SAMHSA's National Outcomes domains
(SAMHSA `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0156" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2003`</xref>`{=html})
defined retention as the length of stay from the date of the first
service to the date of the last service. This definition was only used
in seven of the studies. The most common definition of retention was
'continued in treatment at a specific time (e.g., 30 days)' but criteria
varied across studies (e.g., being in treatment at 3 months, 30 days,
6 weeks, or 4 weeks).
```{=html}
</p>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
The most common way to measure this variable was using a dichotomous
measure. The follow‐up periods ranged from 21 days to 10 years. The
retention rates ranged from 25.3% to 90%.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0100">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}3.2.4`</label>`{=html}
```{=html}
<title>
```
Adherence/Engagement
```{=html}
</title>
```
```{=html}
<p>
```
Adherence/engagement was reported in 22 of the papers
(Table `<xref rid="mpr70027-tbl-0006" ref-type="table">`{=html}6`</xref>`{=html}).
Seven studies focused on medication adherence, with four addressing
adherence to methadone or buprenorphine. Regarding the metric used, 10
papers measured it qualitatively as being engaged. However, most of them
(60%) used a quantitative measure, varying from the number of months
attending treatment to the mean number of sessions completed and
establishing different cutoff points (e.g., attending at least three
treatment sessions). Two studies used ad‐hoc scales to evaluate
treatment adherence.
```{=html}
</p>
```
`<table-wrap position="float" id="mpr70027-tbl-0006" content-type="TABLE" orientation="portrait">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}TABLE
6`</label>`{=html}

    ```{=html}
    <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
    ```
    WHOQOL‐BREF.
    ```{=html}
    </td>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
    ```
    Statistically significant difference between groups
    ```{=html}
    </td>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
    ```
    ---
    ```{=html}
    </td>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
    ```
    ---
    ```{=html}
    </td>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    </tr>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    </tbody>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    </table>
    ```
    `<table-wrap-foot id="mpr70027-ntgp-0004">`{=html}`<fn id="mpr70027-note-0005">`{=html}
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Abbreviations: BQOL: brief quality of life interview; Q‐LES‐Q‐SF:
    quality of life enjoyment and satisfaction questionnaire short form;
    QOL‐5: quality of life‐ 5. QOL‐DA: quality of life dementia
    assessment. QOLI: quality of life inventory; QWB‐SA: quality of
    well‐being self‐administered scale. SF‐6D: short‐form six dimension.
    SF‐36: 36‐item short form health survey; WHOQOL: world health
    organization quality of life.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    `</fn>`{=html}`</table-wrap-foot>`{=html}`</table-wrap>`{=html}
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Regarding the metric, the studies in the review utilised
    self‐administered scales, employing a total of 8 different
    instruments. The most frequently used scale was the WHOQOL‐BREF
    (Skevington
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0150" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2004`</xref>`{=html})
    (Figure `<xref rid="mpr70027-fig-0004" ref-type="fig">`{=html}4`</xref>`{=html}),
    which is a shortened version of the
    `<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}World Health Organisation's QOL
    instrument`</italic>`{=html} (WHOQOL). Other scales included the
    QoL‐5 (Lindholt
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0094" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2002`</xref>`{=html}),
    a 5‐item instrument measuring self‐perceived QoL with items relating
    to mental and physical health, relationships, and existential
    aspects, and the Q‐LES‐Q‐SF (Endicott
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0046" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}1993`</xref>`{=html}),
    which employs 16 items to assess QoL across areas of daily
    functioning.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    `<fig position="float" fig-type="FIGURE" id="mpr70027-fig-0004" orientation="portrait">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}FIGURE
    4`</label>`{=html}
    ```{=html}
    <caption>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Scales used for quality of life measures.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    </caption>
    ```
    `<graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="anchor" id="jats-graphic-7" orientation="portrait" xlink:href="MPR-34-e70027-g002.jpg"/>`{=html}`</fig>`{=html}
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Only two instruments were specific to SUD patients: the QOLI (Daley
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0035" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2005`</xref>`{=html})
    and the QoL‐DA (Wan
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0178" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2011`</xref>`{=html}).
    The remainder were designed for the general population (e.g.,
    WHOQOL) or patients with chronic conditions (e.g., Q‐LES‐Q‐SF).
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    The studies measured QoL at least twice, at baseline, with 6 months
    being the most common follow‐up. Most of the studies were conducted
    in outpatient services, with only six being conducted in inpatient
    facilities or including both types.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    `</sec>`{=html}`</sec>`{=html}`</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0130">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}4`</label>`{=html}
    ```{=html}
    <title>
    ```
    Discussion
    ```{=html}
    </title>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    This review examines seven variables commonly used in SUD treatment
    assessment. Our results highlight that a substantial heterogeneity
    in definitions, metrics and criteria used to assess treatment
    efficacy, and effectiveness is still present in the literature.
    Therefore, we consider the implications of the synthesised
    literature, categorising variables into three domains: substance
    use, related to the treatment process, and QoL. The study provides
    general recommendations to achieve a consensus grounded in
    scientific literature.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    `<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0140">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}4.1`</label>`{=html}
    ```{=html}
    <title>
    ```
    Substance Use
    ```{=html}
    </title>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    In agreement with Maisto
    et al. (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0101" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2016`</xref>`{=html})
    and Sliedrecht
    et al. (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0151" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html}),
    the review found a lack of concordance in the definitions of the
    variables. One of the main debates is whether treatment success
    should be established with a criterion of complete abstinence or
    whether a reduction in use constitutes a valid criterion. The
    predominant view in the literature ‐in line with our results‐ is to
    consider total cessation. However, a more nuanced criteria--
    reduction of use‐ is included in some of the studies. In agreement
    with harm reduction frameworks, studies have shown that low‐risk use
    is an acceptable measure of treatment success, being similar to
    abstinence in terms of subsequent normalisation of psychosocial
    functioning (Aldridge
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0004" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2016`</xref>`{=html};
    Kapadia
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0077" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2021`</xref>`{=html};
    Kline‐Simon
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0085" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2013`</xref>`{=html}).
    Also, considering a clinical approach, reductions in use have a
    clear impact on service management and retention rates in different
    populations (Bailey
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0010" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2024`</xref>`{=html};
    Philips and
    Labrow `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0135" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2000`</xref>`{=html}).
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Although 'reduction in use' appears to be a valid indicator of
    treatment effectiveness with notable clinical advantages, our review
    highlights a critical issue: the absence of consensus in the
    specific criteria for reduction of use and the metric used. This
    undermines the construct validity, given the difficulty in ensuring
    that the measurements align with the theoretical concept. Given the
    clinical relevance of this issue, researchers should continue their
    efforts to further investigate this, build a robust evidence base,
    and work toward establishing a consistent definition of what
    constitutes 'reduction in use'.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    In that sense, our review shows that including a categorical
    definition, which divides patients into 'abstinence' or 'relapse'
    simplifies interpretation, analysis and facilitates cross‐study
    comparisons. However, many authors argue that such measurements may
    lead to a loss of information (Moon and
    Lee `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0117" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2020`</xref>`{=html};
    Witkiewitz
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0184" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2019`</xref>`{=html}).
    Also, this dichotomous classification lacks discrimination, as it
    classified individuals who have reduced substance use and are at
    relatively low risk as 'relapsing'---equating them with those who
    have resumed harmful pre‐treatment use levels (Maisto
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0101" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2016`</xref>`{=html};
    Van Hagen
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0168" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2019`</xref>`{=html}).
    To maintain the advantages of categorical definitions while
    addressing this limitation, we propose a three‐tier classification:
    slip, relapse and abstinence. Differentiating between these groups
    allows for a more nuanced understanding of recovery.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Another methodological issue to consider is the instrument used.
    Most of the studies included self‐report measures, which are
    susceptible to retrospective bias (Van Der Woerd
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0167" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2010`</xref>`{=html})
    or social desirability (Frimpong
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0055" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2016`</xref>`{=html}).
    However, widely used instruments, such as the TLFB---the most used
    instrument of the present review‐ or the ASI, have been reported to
    have good validity (Fals‐Stewart et al. 2000; Ljungvall
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0095" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2020`</xref>`{=html};
    McCann
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0112" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2024`</xref>`{=html}).
    Additionally, it is cost‐effective and straightforward to implement
    (Hjorthoj
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0070" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2012`</xref>`{=html}).
    The ASI has also been translated into various languages (Sharma
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0148" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html}; W.
    Luo
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0099" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2012`</xref>`{=html})
    and tested in patients with psychiatric comorbidities (Susukida
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0157" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2020`</xref>`{=html}),
    providing evidence of external validity. However, it would be
    advisable to include some bias measures.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Biological measures are other of the most common metrics used. These
    methods are considered well‐established with high accuracy and
    reliability (Donovan
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0041" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2012`</xref>`{=html}).
    A principal disadvantage was the short detection window, except for
    hair detection (Bharat
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0015" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2023`</xref>`{=html};
    Donovan
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0041" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2012`</xref>`{=html}).
    Additionally, preventing test adulteration requires significant
    resources. In a recent meta‐analysis about the level of agreement
    between self‐reports and biological measures, the authors concluded
    that the level of agreement was mostly excellent (Bharat
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0015" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2023`</xref>`{=html}).
    Considering this, standardised scales appear to be the most accurate
    methods for measuring substance use. However, when resources allow,
    it is advisable to complement these tools with biological measures
    to enhance the accuracy of the assessment.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Finally, another methodological issue that could affect the
    comparison across‐study was the period of study. The most common was
    the last 30 days. However, there was a great variety between daily
    measurements and the last 8 months. Evidently, the utilisation of a
    measure from the previous month or last 8 months does not yield
    equivalent results. In that sense, the results of the effectiveness
    of treatments could be biased, as the construction we are trying to
    measure could change with time.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Considering these methodological issues, Moe
    et al. (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0116" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html})
    point to different recommendations that should be considered: First,
    the patient's objective (abstinence or reduction in consumption)
    should be assessed; second, it is important to include the
    difference between 'slip' and 'relapse' and finally, the use of
    self‐report measures. As noted by Perestelo‐Perez
    et al. (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0133" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2011`</xref>`{=html}),
    a shared decision with the patient---in terms of whether pursue
    abstinence or just a reduction of substance use‐ may be a promising
    strategy for the achievement of treatment goals. In that sense,
    measures should align with it: for harm reduction or well‐being,
    assess consumption reduction, whereas for full recovery, abstinence
    should be the target (Van Den Brink
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0169" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2006`</xref>`{=html}).
    Second, regarding the difference between 'slip' and 'relapse', it is
    important to make this distinction to identify patients who have
    experienced a slip in the recovery process---which is considered a
    normal part of recovery (Moe
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0116" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html})---versus
    those who have returned to previous patterns of use. Finally,
    self‐report measures are widely, valid and reliable used to assess
    substance use. Additionally, bias measures should be included and,
    to avoid memory bias, some diary measures should be considered.
    Regarding the time of assessment, researchers should continue
    investigations to achieve a consensus.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Finally, it is important to assess not only drug use but also other
    aspects, as recovery from addiction cannot be reduced to a measure
    of use (Martinelli
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0109" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2023`</xref>`{=html}).
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    `</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0150">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}4.2`</label>`{=html}
    ```{=html}
    <title>
    ```
    Related to Treatment (Readmission, Dropout, Retention, and
    Adherence/Engagement)
    ```{=html}
    </title>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Our review found that
    `<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}Readmission`</italic>`{=html} is
    primarily studied within hospital settings and is commonly used to
    assess healthcare quality (Durbin
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0043" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2007`</xref>`{=html};
    Rumball‐Smith and
    Hider `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0144" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2009`</xref>`{=html}).
    However, several methodological issues were noted. First, our
    review, in line with Rumball‐Smith and
    Hider (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0144" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2009`</xref>`{=html}),
    shows that there is a lack of consensus in the operational criteria.
    While most of them referred to it as being hospitalised again, some
    included substance‐related hospitalisation, whereas others counted
    all types of readmissions, which could influence reported rates
    (Fischer
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0053" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2014`</xref>`{=html}).
    For example, including all types of readmissions could lead to an
    overestimation of the readmission rate.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    The 'time window' for measuring readmission also varied from 30 days
    to 9 years. Short windows may miss related readmissions (Fischer
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0053" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2014`</xref>`{=html};
    Rumball‐Smith and
    Hider `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0144" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2009`</xref>`{=html}).
    Most of them used an electronic record, however, the absence of data
    on readmission to different hospitals may lead to underreporting, as
    patients readmitted to other facilities may not be monitored
    (Fischer
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0053" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2014`</xref>`{=html}).
    In this sense, the use of general electronic health records with an
    anonymous patient code could lead to the researcher being informed
    of a patient's readmissions in different settings.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    There is also debate about whether readmission is a good indicator
    of the effectiveness of treatment. Repeated readmissions cause a
    high burden on healthcare systems and patients (Jencks
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0075" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2009`</xref>`{=html}).
    However, some authors have pointed out that from a clinician's point
    of view, early readmission, especially for patients with SUDs, could
    help prevent further deterioration of the medical and social
    situation (Böckmann
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0019" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2019`</xref>`{=html}).
    In such cases, readmission may reflect patients proactively seeking
    support and anticipating a potential relapse. Future research should
    explore the relationships between readmission and different
    treatment aspects, such as relapse and QoL. Moreover, to standardise
    the measure, a new admission should only be considered a new
    substance‐related readmissions.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    `<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}Dropout`</italic>`{=html} was a
    consistent measure across studies. The criteria were generally
    defined as 'stopping treatment before clinical recommendation'.
    Normally, establishing a dichotomised measure. However, there are
    some inconsistencies, with studies including 'being expelled from
    inpatient services' as dropping out (Padyab
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0128" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2015`</xref>`{=html};
    Tull
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0165" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2013`</xref>`{=html}),
    and some have established different cutoff points, for example 'no
    longer in care at the end of a 3‐month' (Bourion‐Bédès
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0020" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2020`</xref>`{=html}).
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Dropout is sometimes seen as synonymous with treatment failure, even
    when clients may have reasonable positive outcomes. It might be
    useful to understand how drop‐out is used in other health settings,
    such as when patients leave primary care after feeling better
    (Walker `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0176" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2009`</xref>`{=html}).
    Combining drop‐out with other aspects could provide a more
    comprehensive understanding of recovery.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    `<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}Retention`</italic>`{=html} is widely
    recognised as an indicator of treatment quality (Dennis
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0038" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2020`</xref>`{=html};
    Schulte
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0147" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2010`</xref>`{=html}),
    and it is correlated with many positive long‐term outcomes,
    including mental health improvements and reduced drug use (Basu
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0011" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2017`</xref>`{=html};
    Gallefoss
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0057" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html};
    Lynch
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0100" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2021`</xref>`{=html}).
    Given its predictive value, retention has evolved from a solid
    predictor to an outcome itself, becoming a primary treatment goal
    (Gallefoss
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0057" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html};
    Schulte
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0147" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2010`</xref>`{=html};
    Walker `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0176" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2009`</xref>`{=html}).
    Some authors suggest that retention should be studied with session
    attendance, as it provides more information on the therapeutic
    process (Pulford
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0138" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2010`</xref>`{=html};
    Viera
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0171" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2020`</xref>`{=html}).
    Only four of the studies included in the review used a combined
    measure (Galvin
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0058" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2020`</xref>`{=html};
    Kraemer
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0086" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2019`</xref>`{=html};
    Orocio‐Contreras and
    Nieto‐Caraveo `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0125" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html};
    Wakeman
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0174" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html}).
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    `<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}Adherence`</italic>`{=html} is also
    related to positive post‐treatment outcomes, such as QoL (Gainer
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0056" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2023`</xref>`{=html};
    Lynch
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0100" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2021`</xref>`{=html}).
    However, adherence has been inconsistently defined across studies.
    Most studies in our review used a dichotomised measure (adherence
    vs. no adherence) which could cause a loss of information and
    statistical power (Burgette and
    Paddock `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0028" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2017`</xref>`{=html};
    Tueller
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0163" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2016`</xref>`{=html}).
    However, dichotomised measures make it easier for clinicians to
    interpret the results and facilitate decision‐making (DeCoster
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0036" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2009`</xref>`{=html}).
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Determining the appropriate cutoff point for retention and adherence
    is a challenge. Dacosta‐Sánchez
    et al. (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0033" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html})
    found that a 3‐month retention period was the best predictor of
    treatment discharge, which aligns with Orocio‐Contreras and
    Nieto‐Caraveo (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0125" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html})
    findings that 90 days in treatment is related to better treatment
    results. Regarding attendance, Dacosta‐Sánchez
    et al. (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0033" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html})
    reported that the proportion of session attendance was the best
    predictor of successful discharge without readmission. However, none
    of the studies in the review used the proportion of session
    attendance as the definition of engagement.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    We recommend using a dichotomised measure of retention (at least
    3 months) alongside session attendance proportion (number of
    attended appointments/number of appointments) as the criteria for
    this domain. A comprehensive analysis of treatment process,
    considering factors such as readmissions, dropouts, and retention,
    along with adherence to the treatment program, will offer a clearer
    picture of future treatment effectiveness. However, it is important
    to note that these are indicators of the treatment process and
    should be evaluated with other domains (e.g., QoL) to determine
    treatment effectiveness.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    `</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0160">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}4.3`</label>`{=html}
    ```{=html}
    <title>
    ```
    Quality of Life (QoL)
    ```{=html}
    </title>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Incorporating QoL measures into SUD treatment services is
    increasingly important for reflecting the patient's perspective on
    their recovery process
    (Laudet `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0090" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2011`</xref>`{=html};
    Kelley and
    Incze `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0080" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2023`</xref>`{=html}).
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    However, findings in QoL studies are inconsistent and difficult to
    interpret due to variations in methodologies, instruments, domains,
    and populations (Morgan
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0120" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2004`</xref>`{=html}).
    These findings are in line with our results. However, all of them
    used self‐administered scales, and the most common was the
    WHOQOL‐BREF. The WHOQOL instrument provides a valuable tool for
    capturing multiple dimensions of QoL (Harper and
    Power `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0068" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}1998`</xref>`{=html};
    Wong
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0185" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2018`</xref>`{=html}).
    The WHOQOL, as well as the short version, has demonstrated robust
    psychometric properties in the general population, making it a
    reliable and valid tool (Bratu
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0022" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2023`</xref>`{=html}).
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    It is also important to use an instrument that assesses different
    areas, apart from physical domains, as individuals with SUD often
    experience a decline in general QoL (Birkeland
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0016" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2018`</xref>`{=html};
    Kim
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0084" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2020`</xref>`{=html}).
    In these senses, the WHOQOL emphasises four areas: physical,
    psychological, and social problems and environmental factors.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    In our review, most of the studies included were performed in the
    last 10 years, demonstrating the recent incorporation of diverse
    recovery metrics beyond 'abstinence.' These are more focused on the
    patient's perception of incorporation and satisfaction with the
    process and well‐being.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    `</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0170">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}4.4`</label>`{=html}
    ```{=html}
    <title>
    ```
    Limitations
    ```{=html}
    </title>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    This review has certain limitations, particularly the initial
    selection of a particular set of variables. The selected variables
    reflect commonly used variables consistently used across studies,
    thus enabling the comparison and synthesis of information. However,
    this strategy left out other potentially significant treatment
    outcomes (e.g., employment, family and social status, overdose)
    which precluded us from expanding the conclusions of the present
    study. However, the outcomes included cover three domains that have
    been identified as the primary focus of treatment efficacy and
    effectiveness measurement in the extant literature (substance use,
    QoL and treatment indicators). Moreover, it is important to consider
    other approaches to obtain a complete view of the evaluation of
    treatment.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    In that sense, some expert consensus has been reached. The most
    recent study was performed by Karnik
    et al. (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0079" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html}),
    whose aim was to reach a consensus on the evaluation of opioid use
    disorder (OUD) treatment. After a Delphi consensus study, they
    proposed the use of five items related to five domains, most of
    which are in line with this review's recommendations. First, a 'drug
    use' item---defined as having two negative screenings within 21
    days---was employed. While this measure may be useful for
    clinicians, we recommend that, from a research perspective, TLFB
    measures of substance use be included for longitudinal assessments,
    as they are cost‐effective. Second, the duration of treatment was
    considered the number of continuous days in treatment (not more than
    30 days from the last expected day of dosing medication). However,
    our review focused on all SUDs (except for tobacco) ‐which are not
    always treated by medication‐, so we proposed the assessment of
    duration by a dichotomous measure (being in treatment for at least
    3 months) and included adherence (by the proportion of appointment
    attendance). This consensus also includes nonfatal overdose and
    fatal opioid poisoning to assess the worsening consequences of drug
    use. Finally, they included an item on patients' global impression
    of improvement, which could be useful in terms of time and resource
    costs for clinicians. However, if possible, we encourage the use of
    a QoL measure for research purposes.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Additionally, other instruments, such as the QoL10 (Muller and
    Clausen `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0121" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2015`</xref>`{=html}),
    the InterRAI quality of life survey (H. Luo
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0098" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2021`</xref>`{=html}),
    and the `<italic toggle="yes">`{=html}Time to Relapse
    Questionnaire`</italic>`{=html} (Adinoff
    et al. `<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0002" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2010`</xref>`{=html}),
    have shown strong reliability and validity in predicting variables
    such as relapse.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Moreover, taking in consideration other limitations, only one author
    performed screening for inclusion and exclusion criteria, which may
    have increased the risk of potential selection errors, although all
    uncertain cases were discussed with two other researchers. Also, the
    bias in the included studies or the methodologies used were not
    included as a criterion for the selection.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Notwithstanding these limitations, the present systematic review
    synthesises the extant literature on seven variables associated with
    SUD treatments evaluation, highlighting significant inconsistencies
    in definitions and methodologies and offering recommendations to
    enhance standardisation.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    `</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0180">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}4.5`</label>`{=html}
    ```{=html}
    <title>
    ```
    Recommendations
    ```{=html}
    </title>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Based on the results of the present review, we recommend
    incorporating to outcome evaluation relevant metrics and clearly
    defined criteria for each of the three identified areas: substance
    use, treatment process, and QoL.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    For substance use, we suggest the implementation of a self‐report
    scale (e.g., TLFB or ASI) in combination---when feasible‐ with a
    biological measure. A collaborative goal‐based approach between
    patients and clinicians concerning the reduction or cessation of
    substance use is also recommended. Future research should aim to
    establish a standardised cut‐off point to differentiate between a
    slip and a relapse and determine an appropriate follow‐up time.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    For the treatment process, the retention of patients for a minimum
    of 3 months in treatment, combined with adherence as measured by
    proportion of attendance, is proposed. These measures provide
    insight into the patient's engagement with the treatment program.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Finally, as noted by Kelley and
    Incze (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0080" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2023`</xref>`{=html}),
    we recommend including a QoL assessment (e.g., WHOQOL‐BREF) to
    assess a general outcome of the patient's perception of recovery or,
    as proposed by Karnik
    et al. (`<xref rid="mpr70027-bib-0079" ref-type="bibr">`{=html}2022`</xref>`{=html}),
    at least one item of patient global impression of improvement,
    particularly in clinical settings where time and resources may be
    limited.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
    `</sec>`{=html}`</sec>`{=html}`<sec id="mpr70027-sec-0190">`{=html}`<label>`{=html}5`</label>`{=html}
    ```{=html}
    <title>
    ```
    Conclusions
    ```{=html}
    </title>
    ```
    ```{=html}
    <p>
    ```
    Establishing standardised measures is vital for improving the
    consistency, effectiveness, and overall understanding of SUD
    treatments. To this end, the present review makes specific
    recommendations that may contribute to paving the way for future
    consensus‐building efforts that will advance the field. This
    presents an initial step in the process of the standardisation of
    outcome‐related metrics and criteria and can inform the design of
    future studies.
    ```{=html}
    </p>
    ```
